FILE: 4_Psychographic_Research_Lesson_DOC__Claude_Deep_Resear_13.txt
================================================================================

﻿I am writing some sales copy targeted towards a specific demographic.

I’ve had multiple LLMs generate Deep Research reports for me on this demographic, and I am attaching each of those as a PDF here. 

What I would like your help with is to go through these reports and then create one unified report that I can use as a reference. 

For greater context, here is the prompt I give to LLMs for deep research. 

I'd like your help doing psychographic research. What are their struggles and pain points? What are their beliefs? 

Below is a series of questions I used in the past, as part of my RMBC Method for copywriting, where the R stands for research. This should be a good framework to use while doing research on the demo. I'd love it if you can even provide 'quotes' from folks in this demo by looking at comments on social media, or in forums, etc. Basically we want to hear what they're saying and what they believe. We want to hear answers in their own words!

Here’s the list of questions:

Insights Into Demographic : 

Who Is Your Customer?

What Attitudes Do They Have? (Religious, Political, Social, Economic)?

What Are Their Hopes and Dreams?

What Are Their Victories and Failures?

What Outside Forces Do THEY Believe Have Prevented Their Best Life?

What Are Their Prejudices?

Sum Up Their Core Beliefs About Life, Love, and Family In 1-3 Sentences. 

Other Existing Solutions: 

What is the Market Already Using? (List Out)

What Has Their Experience Been Like?

What Does the Market Like About Existing Solutions?

What Does the Market Dislike About Existing Solutions?

Are Their Horror Stories About Existing Solutions?

Does the Market Believe Existing Solution Works?

If Not, Why?

Curiosity:
Has Someone Tried to Solve the Market’s Pain Points Before In A Very Unique Way? 

What Was The Result?

Is There A Conspiratorial Story Behind Why Old Solutions Didn’t Work?
Are There Any Older Attempts to Solve the Problem (Pre-1960) That Are Unique?

What Happened? Were they successful but forgotten? Or were they a failure? Why?

Examples: 
1) Tesla in the energy space. Big energy didn’t want his solutions, he was discredited and shamed. His inventions and discoveries were thrown into the ash heap of history until now.  
2) U.S. Army tried to cure foot fungus during WWII, surgeon general was on a desperate race because troops were missing service time. Finally succeeded by using Undecylenic Acid. But today we forget how effective it is. 

Corruption:
Is There A Belief That the Market’s Pain Point Used To Not Exist, Or Used To Not Be So Bad?

Is There A Belief That It’s Been Recently Exacerbated By Outside Forces?

If So, What Are Those Forces And What’s The Reason Behind Their Presence?

Examples: 
Obesity and diabetes being the result of Dr. Ancil Keyes. 

This isolated group of people doesn’t struggle from whatever condition/pain point that most of us do. In America we DO suffer from this pain point. The reason why is that we are exposed to these outside forces while this isolated group isn’t.

========

Please go ahead and create the document now.

﻿Prompt: 

Thanks. Now please continue deep research and leverage social signals like social media, forums, etc to provide the following:
1. As many quotes as possible from women describing their specific pain points around aging skin, looking older, wrinkles, and so on. I want to hear how they think about the specific pain points here in their own words!

Example #1

I am writing some sales copy targeted towards U.S. women 40 + who have wrinkles, dark spots, or puffy eye bags. I'd like your help doing psychographic research. What are their struggles and pain points? What are their beliefs? 

Below is a series of questions I used in the past, as part of my RMBC Method for copywriting, where the R stands for research. This should be a good framework to use while doing research on the demo. I'd love it if you can even provide 'quotes' from folks in this demo by looking at comments on social media, or in forums, etc. Basically we want to hear what they're saying and what they believe. We want to hear answers in their own words!

Here’s the list of questions:

Insights Into Demographic : 

Who Is Your Customer?

What Attitudes Do They Have? (Religious, Political, Social, Economic)?

What Are Their Hopes and Dreams?

What Are Their Victories and Failures?

What Outside Forces Do THEY Believe Have Prevented Their Best Life?

Sum Up Their Core Beliefs About Life, Love, and Family In 1-3 Sentences.

What Has Their Experience Been Like?

What Does the Market Dislike About Existing Solutions?

What Happened? Were they successful but forgotten? Or were they a failure? Why?

Is There A Belief That the Market’s Pain Point Used To Not Exist, Or Used To Not Be So Bad?

Is There A Belief That It’s Been Recently Exacerbated By Outside Forces?

If So, What Are Those Forces And What’s The Reason Behind Their Presence?

Example: 
This isolated group of people doesn’t struggle from whatever condition/pain point that most of us do. In America we DO suffer from this pain point. The reason why is that we are exposed to these outside forces while this isolated group isn’t.

Increasing Scale and Profitability with Conversion Revenue Optimization
Ethan Bence

Which Version Won?

v3

by 34%

Updated by 37%

This is why we perform CRO and split-tests

Super Fast About Us
Ethan Bence, Founder of 4080 Marketing
From Pittsburgh, PA originally
E-commerce + Info Product CRO Team focused on increasing profitability and scale through split-testing
Started with Facebook Ads in 2015 and got into CRO shortly after
Business + Statistics Degree from Carnegie Mellon (aka math nerd)
Work(ed) with brands like Zumba Fitness, GetThin MD, MenoLabs, Crio Bru, Mastering.com, and hundreds of others
We help generate $10m incremental revenue per year via split-testing
We’re Business Owners → Marketers → CRO Practitioners

Agenda
Overall Goal: Give you an initial framework + real test examples and ideas to start your CRO journey to be able to increase your conversion rates, average order value, and profits throughout 2024

Introduction to Conversion Rate Optimization (and why Conversion Revenue Optimization is superior)
Split-testing - why it is a requirement, the ins and outs
How to come up with and decide which tests to run
Examples real-life winning tests + why they worked
Q&A

Introduction to traditional CRO - 
Why is it Important?

What effects does your business feel if you increase your overall conversion rate by 15%?

Introduction to traditional CRO - 
Why is it Important?

More competition in most niches
Rising ad costs
Higher importance on profitability and spend efficiency
Makes you resistant to scale and seasonality

Introduction to traditional CRO - 
Conversion Rate Optimization
What do you think CRO entails?

Introduction to traditional CRO - 
Conversion Rate Optimization
What do YOU think CRO entails?
Most people say: changing button colors, split-testing CTA text, changing fonts, etc.
These tests require a LOT of sessions to attain statistically significant results - and typically, the CVR increases are small

In our opinion, this is the WRONG way to handle CRO (very, very, very wrong)...

Most brands don’t even do CRO AT ALL. Even the big ones. Something is better than nothing. There are other common problems: 
Increasing CVR without factoring in AOV is pointless
“Let’s have a 99% off sale”
Clearly, there needs to be a better way to make traffic convert more and buy more…
Introduction to traditional CRO (cont.)
    Test Slowly for 2.5% increase

Introducing Conversion REVENUE Optimization

We drive large increases in our golden metric: Earnings per Session (more on that in a second)
We take a direct response marketing approach combined with UX improvements

We do this by: Creating irresistible offers,
Using direct response copywriting that speaks about the customer; not the brand,
Looking for hidden market segments/avatars that buy without us marketing to them.

We’re more concerned with driving greater increases faster than knowing exactly why our test worked/failed
It’s a faster route to statistical significance
Improve conversion rate AND the revenue that it drives

Conversion REVENUE Optimization - EPS
Now, we need to have a metric to track and optimize. Our golden metric: 
Earnings per Session
= Conversion Rate x Average Order Value
= the average amount of revenue we expect from one session on our site 
AKA Revenue per Visitor, Earnings per Click (slightly different metric, but sometimes people mix them together)
Example: 3.5% CVR x $49 AOV = $1.71 EpS 

Conversion REVENUE Optimization - Split Testing
We send a % of traffic to our current control
We send a % of traffic to a version with improvements we think will convert better
We track the conversions and revenue from each
Conceptually, it’s that simple.

What it Looks Like

Conversion REVENUE Optimization - Incremental Revenue
Our success is mostly determined by one metric: Incremental Revenue
It’s one of the few things we can point to in business that we know we caused an improvement, and by how much
Incremental Revenue = the additional revenue we get by having a better converting experience

Increases to Earnings per Session create Incremental Revenue

Introduction to Split-Testing
90%+ brands don’t split- test properly!
Running 2 Ad Sets to one page and 2 to another page is NOT a split-test
Running 1 landing page in October and another in January is NOT a split-test

Introduction to Split-Testing - Controlling Variables
As much as we can, we need to control for variables like:
Traffic quality, Seasonality, and Time of week

Imagine running one variation during BFCM and one during July!?

We want both runners to run in the rain or the sun

Introduction to Split-Testing - Controlling Variables
Avoid making a change to a page, getting poor quality traffic, and incorrectly concluding your change made it worse 

“Our conversion rate dropped as soon as we changed the site!”

Split Testing solves that

Introduction to Split-Testing
Split-Testing is like having a do-over button. There’s no need to guess!

Imagine that in real-life?!

Split-Testing Infrastructure
Split Testing Software
Convert.com, VWO, Intelligems, Optimizely
Heatmaps
Heatmap.com, Hotjar, Lucky Orange
Tagging for LTV
Hyros, CRM, etc

… So What do We Test?
CRO is a double edged sword
There are endless options to test!
…. But… there are endless options to test
Multiple frameworks you can use
Whichever one gets you testing fastest is generally the best
Inside-Out CRO is a way to prioritize your tests via Bottleneck Reduction
It takes the guesswork out of what to test and when

Inside Out CRO - Ecommerce Example
Upsells
Checkout
Cart
Sales pages/Landing Pages
Product Pages
Collection Pages
Homepage
Banner
Navigation
Menu

Bottleneck Analysis
We want to first optimize the points that everyone must go through
Like airport security
Same process for other funnel structures
Call funnel, webinar funnel, quiz funnel, etc.
The best plan is the one you follow

Testing Philosophy
Bigger changes = better chance at a significant result (good or bad)
Don’t do a button color test…
CRO Priority #1 -> make sure we don’t HARM conversion rate
The customer’s opinion is what we care about most
What people say they care about when asked directly is often what they think they should say
Get “paid” while we learn

Test Ideation
Now let’s talk about researching and creating tests…

Test Ideation
Rule Number #1: TALK TO YOUR CUSTOMERS
Phone calls
Texts
Customer support emails
Reviews
Competitor Reviews
Customer feedback surveys
Manual = better than automated

Test Ideation
Rule Number #1: TALK TO YOUR CUSTOMERS

Do nothing but this and you will get 80%+ of total gains

Test Ideation
Rule Number #1: TALK TO YOUR CUSTOMERS
Phone calls are the best
Customers don’t have a ton of time to prepare a “canned” response
You’re more likely to get their true feelings
Listen “between the lines”
Listen for feelings and emotion
Topical/relevant items
Back to school? Something their kids are doing?
Listen for repeated wording/phrases

Test Ideation - Know Your Customer

Test Ideation - Know Your Customer

Test Ideation - Heatmaps

Test Ideation
Scroll Depth

Test Ideation
Scroll Depth

Test Ideation - Scroll Depth
Takeaway: your above the fold MATTERS
You can spend months testing your above the fold
Above the fold gets attention
But… below the fold converts people

Test Ideation - Pitfalls
Do not assume that just because competitors are running something that it’s working
Good design =/= high converting design
Do NOT just implement something live because “it’s surely better”
YOU are NOT the customer
Don’t “armchair” CRO
“Well actually… I like this design better personally”

Types of Tests
Copy
Design
Offer
Avatar/market segment
Funnel structure (quiz, advertorial, etc.)
Tech Stack Test
“Business Level” Test
You can pair most of these with the “Inside Out” Funnel
Upsell + Copy Test
Sales Page + Offer Test
Product Page + Design Test

Enough Theory - Let’s Go Over Some Winning Tests of Each Type & What’s Working Today

Copy Test

Copy Test

Copy Test
6.4% Increase
“Take Control” language won
Headlines are very tough to predict which will win
There’s not a great way other than testing a large volume of headlines

Design Test
32% Increase to RPV
Images are worth a thousand words
Don’t link to socials in your menu
Info architecture
Eliminate unnecessary choices

Test

Design Test
10% Increase to RPV
Informed by Heatmaps

This is why you can’t just go by “best practices”
More emphasis on meals and experience

12.3% increase to RPV
Customers care about “certified”

Always iterate on your winners
Times are always changing
Sometimes, we let our designers have fun

Design 
Test

Copy Test +
Design

Copy + Design Test
17% increase to CVR
Seasonal relevance
Conversion rate matters more to this business due to a backend monthly recurring membership

Avatar Test

Updated image won by 34%
Aspirational vs. Inspirational
Stefan, Ben, and I waxing poetic
Avatar Test

Design + Copy Test + Business Test

Increased RPV by 30%
Making it feel more product focused
Leaning in to social proof
Compliant version of page
Design + Copy + Business Test

Funnel Test + Offer ($49 vs. $17 product)
Goal to get maximum amount of members
$49 frontend product vs. $17 frontend product
Was breaking even on both
Common sense would say $49 would ascend better to membership…
Ascend at the exact same rate
Same LTV

Offer Test - Mix and Match vs PDP
CVR +2.88%
RPV -7.76%
But… Subscriptions increased 467%

Funnel Test - 
Advertorial

Funnel Test (Advertorial)
Flat conversion rate compared to mix and match
It’s a CRO “loss” but a marketing win
We have different places to send traffic to
Easier to scale with multiple entry points
Better for algorithm
Funnel portfolio theory

Offer Test - Pricing ($47 vs. $97 VIP for Live Event)
45% Increase in Conversion Rate
41% Decrease in Price/AOV
VIP purchase highly correlated with backend high-ticket sales
*not for every business
Higher conversion rate -> more buyers -> more backend sales

Tech Stack Test - WebinarJam vs. StealthSeminar
19% Increase in Leads
38% Increase in Calls
35% Increase in Sales
No copy or design changes

“Business” Test - Payment Processor
Business wanted to move to a different payment processor for better terms
Marketing + Ops + Finance all have a stake in the performance
~0% change
Split-testing can be used as an “insurance” policy
Can also be used to put a $ to how much a change will cost you

“Business” Test - Accept SMS Terms before Opt In 
Business wanted to test accepting SMS terms during opt in at recommendation of legal
22% Drop in Rev per Visitor
Implementing would have cost ~$260k per month
Split-testing can be used as an “insurance” policy

The Importance of Losing Tests
“Losing” test doesn’t always mean you lost
Usually it means “tied”/“didn’t win”
We turn losers off after 7 days
We let winners run for months/years (until we beat it again)

Odds and Ends
Let tests run for 7 days to call a loser, 14 to call a winner (at least one business cycle)
85%+ chance to win
Tests will flip-flop the first few days - resist the urge to mess with them

Action Steps/Takeaways Before Q&A
Start testing ASAP using Inside Out
Test with software
Get one test live by end of next week, even if it’s just a headline test
It’ll take you 10 minutes
Talk to your customers
When in doubt, test it out
Your takeaways?

﻿I’m working on a marketing campaign in _NICHE_. The product is _PRODUCT_ and we focus on customers in _REGION_. 

So far I have performed research around Product/Market Awareness Levels, Competitors, and Psychographic Research.

I am going to upload several PDFs that contain this research. 

What I want you to do is go through these PDFs in-depth and then create one unified research brief that contains all of the key elements that would be needed in order to successfully implement this campaign. Specifically, this will be a unified document that I will share with you and other LLMs for background information and context as we generate additional copy and marketing assets, most of which will be direct response oriented. 

I don’t need information on TAM size, or even specific strategies that were included in these deep research reports. Instead what I really need are the following:

1. Target Market Demographic Overview
Focus on people who fall into the Problem Aware and Solution Aware Market segments primarily. 

2. Target Market Psychographic Overview
What are their problems and pain points? 

[Examples, from a DIFFERENT PRODUCT in a DIFFERENT NICHE]

Feel embarrassed, ashamed and humiliated about their weight, especially when comparing themselves to thinner women.
* Struggle to lose weight with diet and exercise. Get frustrated when the weight comes back after losing a few pounds.
* Feel like a prisoner in their body and that their weight is destined to keep increasing.
* Experience low self-esteem, lack of confidence, feeling unattractive.
* Health and mobility issues like low energy, trouble moving, joint pain.
* Judged and stared at by others for their weight. Feel they are a burden.
* Clothes don't fit right anymore. Can't fit into "skinny" jeans.
* Fear their partner no longer finds them attractive.
* Can't keep up with kids/grandkids or do activities they enjoy.

What are their hopes and dreams?
(Specifically, what does their ideal future state look like where their problem has been solved. What would that look like? How would they look and feel? When they dream and imagine this future, what else is true about their life at this point?)

How do they view themselves more broadly, what language should we use when speaking to them, and what language should we avoid?

What are the Primary Promises we can make to them, and that they would want to hear, when it comes to solving their pain points? 

[Examples, from a DIFFERENT PRODUCT in a DIFFERENT NICHE]

* Experience Rapid fat loss from belly, hips, thighs without extreme diet or exercise.
* Lose up to 71 lbs of fat fast.
* Boost metabolism and burn fat 24/7 by activating "fat cell hypothermia".
* Increase confidence, self-esteem, feeling sexy and vibrant again.
* Fit back into favorite clothes like jeans, dresses, and swimwear.
* Regain independence and stop feeling held back by weight.
* Be admired, desired, and turn heads again.
* Reclaim energy, youthfulness and love life again.
* Free yourself from guilt, shame and worry about your weight.

3. What will their biggest objections be to our marketing campaigns for our product, and how can we address those?

4. What existing solutions has the market tried already? Why are those existing solutions not adequate enough?

The LIFT Method: Boost Your Front End VSL Conversions by 50%-100% in One Scientific Test
For DR Marketers the Financial Opportunity Space Who Want Bigger Scale and Margins…
Without Overhauling Your Fulfillment, Hurting Your AOV or Causing a Refund Spike (Works for Low- and High-ticket Funnels)

How Well Does It Work?
I’ve used this process to help my clients…
Take a $97 offer selling “high-ticket closing” from 10 buyers a day to 480 buyers a day 
High-ticket call funnel from $80,713 per month in spend at 1.57 ROAs to $198,959 in monthly spend at 2.57 ROAs (now the biggest airbnb offer)
Course on real estate wholesaling that had never worked on cold to $16K/day in spend 

There’s more… 
Another call funnel from $175,258 per month in gross profit to $791,897 per month 
Got an 87% lift for the biggest offer in the credit repair space ($39 offer from 1.65% CVR to 3.08%)
A course on tax lien investing: $5K/day in spend to $15K/day
$97 offer on how to become a YouTube media buyer from 0.9% CVR to 2.1%

And more… 
Low ticket front-end selling wholesale real estate: cut the CPA in half 
Revived a “dead” capital brokering biz opp (<$1K/day → $7K+)
Bookkeeping biz opp from zero to 100 buyers a day, break even on cold, before we even had an upsell live
Assorted upsell wins (e.g. 11% lift on an “unbeatable” upsell)
Etc…

What Is The LIFT Method?
	
L –   List Your Assets
	I   –   Identify Your Big Idea(s)
	F –   Frame & Rebuild Your VSL
	T –   Test Your Major Hypotheses

Step 1: List Your Assets
Pour through the control VSL… skim the fulfillment… interview the guru
Hunt for assets 
Promise
Proof
Intrigue
Macro story
Micro story
Market fit
Urgency
An asset is anything your market will find important, exciting or beneficial.

First thing I do is comb through the control, fulfillment, guru’s story, etc. hunting for assets
An asset is anything your market will find important, exciting or beneficial 
They fall into 7 categories 
Promise: how much money I can make, how quickly, how passively, etc
Proof: video testimonials, screenshot wins, bank account screenshare, opportunity demonstrations, speaker credibility, etc
Intrigue: anything that provokes curiosity (loopholes, hacks, tricks, anomalies, etc)
Macro story: story of the opportunity (AI, Amazon, Airbnb, real estate, etc)
Micro story: the hero’s journey
Market fit: this will work for YOU (without, even if, no matter if…) 
Urgency: you need to act now
Some will already be at the top of the marketing
Some will be buried
Examples: PF, 10X, ODF

Some already leveraged, some buried
Examples: PF, 10X, ODF

Step 2: Identify Your Big Idea(s)
Big ideas aren’t magic. They’re just combinations of little ideas.
Most marketers are intimidated by the thought of “coming up with” big ideas, but… 
Recall: your assets = any idea your market will see as important, exciting or beneficial
Promise, Proof, Intrigue, Macro story, Micro story, Market fit, Urgency
Big Idea: A combination of 3–5 powerful assets
How to find them: Combine your assets while asking, “How can I make this more important, exciting and beneficial in the market’s eyes?” 
Action: Jot 1-3 Big Ideas
Most marketers get intimidated by the term “big idea”... sounds like this mystical, nebulous thing that they conjure up on the Agora copy calls and no one but the most senior guys in financial know how to do it 
But it’s simple
A big idea is just a combination of little ideas
It’s a combination of 3-5 powerful marketing assets
Rope analogy
So in step 1 you list your assets, and in step 2 you play with them
You combine your assets in different ways while asking, “How can I make this more important, exciting and beneficial in the eyes of the market?”
Example: affiliate marketing with youtube advertising (YT forward)
Example: AI-made ebooks (AI jailbreak)
Example: MM
You’ll have multiple ideas. No need to commit. Jot them

Rope analogy
Examples: affiliate marketing with youtube advertising (YT forward), AI-made ebooks (AI jailbreak), MM

Step 3: Frame & Rebuild Your VSL
Clarify your market 
Who are we speaking to? 
Identify your median prospect
Structure the VSL (macro + micro story)
Macro story = what you reveal about your opportunity, when and how
Micro story = the hero’s emotional journey
Draft & polish the script
Draft in a day
Edit for a week
Now that you’ve got your assets and 2-3 big ideas knocking around in your head, it’s time to frame and rebuild your VSL
Three substeps 
Clarify your market (median prospect)
Structure your VSL (block of marble becomes the shape of a man)
Draft and polish (chisel until we have the statue of David)

Step 3.1: Clarify Your Market & Median Prospect
You’re in the financial opportunity market
Here’s how it segments:
Two big buckets:
Security-driven: behind on bills, drowning in debt, desperate for breathing room
Independence-driven: hate their 9–5, income capped, want control of their time and income
Age
30s, 40s, 50s, 60+
Gender
Men/women
Awareness around your opportunity 
Unaware, heard of it, looked into it, tried it
Your “median prospect” sits at the statistical center of your target market
What you want to know about them:
Their core problem
How it shows up in their life
How they feel about it (fear, anger, shame, envy, guilt, dread)
The immediate transformation they dream about 
The solutions they’ve heard of, looked into, or tried
What they love and hate about each of those solutions
Their perceived constraints (no time, bad credit, no skills, etc.)
Psychographics:
https://docs.google.com/document/d/1mcgfzi65P9d2FtdcADE3JsmIZg8imLSG9UmW0CVP3eg/edit?usp=sharing 

Step 3.2: Structure Your VSL (Macro & Micro Story) 
Macro story: what you reveal about your opportunity, when and how
Two major questions you need to ask:
What will your prospect be aware of coming out of your lead?
Default towards blind 
What will they need to be aware of before buying?
Blind = lower CPA and LTV
Aware = higher CPA and LTV
Micro story: the hero’s journey you’re pulling the market through
Rags → Failed solutions → Rock bottom → Strange new world → Mentor → Secret → Riches
Outside of burying assets, structure is the #1 thing people screw up
Two layers: Macro and micro
Micro you understand. It’s your rags to riches story
Macro is what no one in biz opp seems to understand
They do a great job teasing out the opportunity in their ads and the first few minutes of the VSL, but then they get to the body and basically hand the market a bowl of information soup and say “down this, then I’ll tell you about my product”
They reveal too much too soon too fast… and leave the market confused
Macro is how we tease out our opportunity
Two questions: prospect awareness coming out of the lead? Awareness before the first CTA?
In the lead, default towards blind (blind = more attention)
Before they buy, odds are you’ll want to reveal some things (aware = better customers)
If you start blind and reveal the right things at the right time in the right way, you get the best of both worlds
Example: O-Farming

Blind = more attn. Aware = better customers. Start blind and reveal correctly = best of both worlds
Example: O-Farming

Step 3.3: Draft & Polish the Script
Order of things
Draft body and offer → Edit to 90% → Draft close → Draft first lead → Polish to 100%
Copywriting principles 
Conversational 
Simple 
Tangible
High momentum

Step 4: Test Your Major Hypotheses
Your big ideas are hypotheses, not certainties
Nobody nails it 100% of the time
If you take 3-5 real swings, you stack the odds of a big lift
Test 3-5 leads
Your primary lead is the one you’re most confident in
1-2 of your alternates hedge your assumptions
And 1-2 are your “Barry Bonds swings”
Each lead = different big idea or treatment 
You’re exploring different ways of combining/weighting your strongest assets:
Blind vs forward
Big macro story at the top
Down the middle claims
Weird angle
Etc 
Bonus: test each lead with its own ads and lander
Congruence = lift 
One offer becomes multiple
Examples: CS, AAN, PF

﻿# 🌍 Income Opportunity Market: Psychographic Profiles
## Security Bucket
### 30s × Security — “Breathing Room”

#### Who they are

Mid–late 30s W-2 workers juggling rising rents, childcare costs, and leftover student debt (often without a degree). Savings are thin to nonexistent; one surprise bill tips the month. Many patch with gig apps or odd shifts, but exhaustion and schedule chaos limit capacity. Employer health coverage is a lifeline. They clicked **Security** because the priority is simple: stop the monthly scramble and breathe.

#### Core problem (in their words)

* “My bills don’t match my paycheck—every month is a fire drill.”
* “One car repair and we’re behind on rent or utilities.”
* “I don’t have spare hours or energy to chase something complicated.”

#### Emotional landscape

* **Fear:** of late fees, eviction notices, and losing coverage.
* **Anger:** at prices rising faster than pay.
* **Shame:** for being “this age” and still scrambling.
* **Disgust:** with predatory fees and debt traps.
* **Guilt:** missing time with kids/partner to pick up extra shifts.
* **Envy:** of peers who seem stable with a cushion.
* **Sadness:** feeling stuck despite working hard.

#### Solutions explored / tried

**Extra shifts / second job (traditional path)**

* *Like:* immediate hours = immediate money; familiar.
* *Hate:* burnout; childcare conflicts; schedule whiplash.

**Gig apps (rideshare, delivery, task gigs)**

* *Like:* quick on/off; cash flow this week.
* *Hate:* low net after costs; unreliable demand; wear and tear.

**Online hustles (reselling, micro-freelance, dropship/print-on-demand)**

* *Like:* home-based; potential to scale.
* *Hate:* steep learning curve; inconsistent results; time sink.

**Debt tactics / quick cash (balance transfers, BNPL, payday, side loans)**

* *Like:* instant relief; buys time.
* *Hate:* fees snowball; future months get worse.

**Assistance / family support (SNAP, church, borrowing)**

* *Like:* safety valve; non-repay grants/food support.
* *Hate:* stigma; limited; strains relationships.

#### Who they blame

* Wages that haven’t kept up with housing/childcare/transport costs; predatory finance.
* Themselves, a little, for past money choices—but mostly the math just doesn’t work.

#### Constraints (real + perceived)

* **Benefits dependence** (can’t risk gaps).
* **Childcare/time scarcity** (irregular schedules).
* **Cash/credit limits** (fees and APRs trap them).
* **Transportation strain** (commute + gig costs).
* **Energy bandwidth** (chronic fatigue).
* **Trust deficit** (skeptical from past tries).
* **Partner alignment** (household tradeoffs are tight).

#### Primary promise

> **End the monthly scramble—create reliable income this month that covers essentials and builds a real buffer, without risking your benefits or stealing family time.**

#### Likely objections

* “Will this put money in the month fast enough?”
* “What hidden costs or gotchas will make next month worse?”
* “I’m already exhausted—how much time/energy does this take?”
* “Is my credit or past mistakes going to block me?”
* “I can’t risk losing health coverage.”
* “I’ve tried things before—why wouldn’t this fizzle too?”
* “My partner won’t sign off on another dead end.”
* “I don’t have reliable childcare/transport—can this work anyway?”
* “Is this legit—or another trap with fees?”

#### Hopes & dreams

* **Short term (30–90 days):**
  * Bills covered on time; late fees stop.
  * First real cushion—$500–$1,000 that stays put.
  * Fewer panicked nights; more presence with kids.
  * Sense of control returning.

* **Long term (12–36 months):**
  * Debts shrinking, credit healing, emergency fund in place.
  * Stable housing (renewed lease or starter home that isn’t crushing).
  * Predictable schedule with family time protected.
  * Confidence: “we’re okay—even if something breaks.”

---

### 40s × Security — “Safety Net Builder”

#### Who they are

Mid–late 40s earners carrying a full load—mortgage or high rent, kids (sometimes adult kids at home), and emerging parent care. Pay feels capped; overtime and side jobs plug holes but drain health and family time. Medical and credit card debt loom. Employer benefits are non-negotiable. They clicked **Security** to stop the slide, cover the gap every month, and rebuild a real safety net.

#### Core problem (in their words)

* “We’re one emergency from falling behind—again.”
* “Extra shifts keep us afloat but leave us wiped out.”
* “At this age I shouldn’t be this fragile—but here we are.”

#### Emotional landscape

* **Fear:** of a major bill knocking everything over.
* **Anger:** at being paid less value than the workload demands.
* **Shame:** for “should’ve had this figured out by now.”
* **Disgust:** at interest, medical billing games, and junk fees.
* **Guilt:** shortchanging kids/parents on time and attention.
* **Envy:** families with savings and dependable slack.
* **Sadness:** grieving the stability they imagined they’d have.

#### Solutions explored / tried

**Extra shifts / job changes (traditional path)**

* *Like:* known path; benefits intact.
* *Hate:* permanent pay cuts on re-entry; burnout; limited upside.

**Patchwork side work (delivery, handyman, seasonal)**

* *Like:* immediate dollars; tangible.
* *Hate:* unpredictable; physically taxing; weekends disappear.

**Online options (micro-business, remote side work)**

* *Like:* hope of recurring income; home-based.
* *Hate:* complexity creep; takes too long to stabilize.

**Debt/relief maneuvers (consolidation, settlements, balance transfers)**

* *Like:* momentary relief; simplifies payments.
* *Hate:* fees, credit impacts, and problems just shift forward.

**Assistance/community (benefits, charity, family loans)**

* *Like:* keeps the lights on; compassionate support.
* *Hate:* paperwork, stigma, strings; not a plan.

#### Who they blame

* Employers/economy that devalue experience and raise costs; medical/insurance complexity.
* Themselves, some, for earlier choices—but mostly the system and bad luck.

#### Constraints (real + perceived)

* **Benefits dependence** (family health coverage).
* **Time squeeze** (kids + parent care + work).
* **Physical wear/energy** (can’t add heavy labor).
* **Credit strain** (limits options, raises costs).
* **Mortgage/transport anchors** (can’t relocate easily).
* **Decision fatigue** (tried so much; trust is thin).
* **Spousal consensus** (must be low-drama and dependable).

#### Primary promise

> **Stabilize the household fast—dependable monthly income that closes the gap, rebuilds a real emergency cushion, and fits your limited time, energy, and benefits.**

#### Likely objections

* “How soon does the money actually show up?”
* “Is this dependable every month—or another spiky side thing?”
* “What’s the tradeoff—will this steal time from my family or health?”
* “Any risk to my benefits or current job?”
* “Hidden fees, credit hits, or fine print I’ll regret?”
* “I can’t handle a steep learning curve.”
* “My partner is done with experiments—how do I prove this is sane?”
* “If something happens with my parents/kids, can this flex?”
* “I’ve been burned—show me real proof, not hype.”

#### Hopes & dreams

* **Short term (30–90 days):**
  * No more juggling which bill to pay; collectors quiet down.
  * First real buffer back in savings.
  * Evenings/weekends partially restored to family.
  * Confidence that the slide has stopped.

* **Long term (12–36 months):**
  * Debts meaningfully reduced; medical balances manageable.
  * A durable 3–6 month cushion; credit repaired.
  * Predictable, humane schedule with bandwidth for family care.
  * The calm of knowing a single setback won’t sink them.

—

### 50s × Security — “Runway Protector”

#### Who they are
Mid-50s long-tenured W-2 workers with mortgages still active and retirement behind plan. Overtime (if available) plugs gaps, but health, energy, and family obligations limit capacity. Rehiring after a layoff likely means lower pay and weaker benefits, so they cling to current employment. They chose **Security** because one more shock could derail their last earning runway.

#### Core problem (in their words)
- “If I’m laid off now, we won’t recover.”
- “We’re house-rich, cash-poor—and one bill away from trouble.”
- “I need extra money that doesn’t risk our benefits or my health.”

#### Emotional landscape
- **Fear:** layoff, medical bills, never catching up before retirement.  
- **Anger:** salaries capped while costs climb.  
- **Shame:** being “this age” and still scrambling.  
- **Disgust:** interest/fee traps; insurance games.  
- **Guilt:** shortchanging spouse/kids/parents on time.  
- **Envy:** peers with pensions or big 401(k)s.  
- **Sadness:** grieving the retirement they imagined.

#### Solutions explored / tried
**Extra shifts / second job**  
- *Like:* immediate, familiar, keeps benefits.  
- *Hate:* burnout; family time gone; limited availability.

**Gig work (delivery/handyman/seasonal)**  
- *Like:* quick cash, flexible start/stop.  
- *Hate:* low net after costs; physical wear; unreliable.

**Online/remote side work**  
- *Like:* home-based; less physical strain.  
- *Hate:* complexity creep; slow to become dependable.

**Debt maneuvers (consolidation, transfers, settlements)**  
- *Like:* temporary relief; simpler payments.  
- *Hate:* fees/credit hits; kicks the can.

**Asset taps (401k loans, HELOC, pawn/sell)**  
- *Like:* fast liquidity.  
- *Hate:* jeopardizes retirement; adds risk and payments.

#### Who they blame
- Employers/economy that target older workers and raise costs; medical/insurance complexity.  
- Themselves a bit for earlier choices—but mostly the system and timing.

#### Constraints (real + perceived)
- **Benefits dependence** (family coverage is non-negotiable).  
- **Energy/health limits** (can’t add heavy labor).  
- **Time squeeze** (work + kids/adult kids + parent care).  
- **Credit strain** (APR/limits raise costs).  
- **Mortgage/transport anchors** (can’t relocate easily).  
- **Trust deficit** (burned by “fixes” that backfire).  
- **Spousal consensus** (must be low-drama and sane).

#### Primary promise
> **Build a dependable second paycheck outside your job—hedge a layoff now, close the monthly gap, and rebuild a real cushion.**

#### Likely objections
- “How fast does money show up—and is it truly monthly?”  
- “Will this threaten my benefits or job?”  
- “I can’t handle a big learning curve or upfront costs.”  
- “What if my health or family needs spike?”  
- “We’ve tried ‘solutions’ before—prove this isn’t another fee trap.”  
- “My spouse is done with experiments—how do I show this is sane?”  
- “I can’t do physical side work—will this still fit?”  
- “Can this flex around overtime and caregiving?”  
- “If I get laid off, does this still help?”

#### Hopes & dreams
- **Short term (30–90 days):**  
  - Bills covered on time; collectors quiet.  
  - First real buffer restored; fewer “what if” spirals.  
  - Evenings/weekends partially returned to family.  
  - Confidence that the slide has stopped.
- **Long term (12–36 months):**  
  - Debts cut down; 3–6 month cushion intact.  
  - Predictable income stream that reduces overtime dependence.  
  - Retirement track looks plausible again.  
  - Calm, dignified stability—even when life throws a curveball.

---

### 60+ × Security — “Fixed-Income Fortifier”

#### Who they are
Mid-60s individuals living on Social Security plus small withdrawals, often adding part-time or gig income to keep up with rising costs. Health and energy vary; hiring doors have mostly closed. Housing and medical expenses dominate decisions. They chose **Security** to keep the lights on without sacrificing dignity or risking health.

#### Core problem (in their words)
- “Prices keep climbing; my check doesn’t.”  
- “I can’t outwork this with my health the way it is.”  
- “I need income that’s steady, low-strain, and won’t jeopardize what I have.”

#### Emotional landscape
- **Fear:** outliving savings; medical shocks.  
- **Anger:** at being sidelined after a lifetime of work.  
- **Shame:** having to keep working or ask family for help.  
- **Disgust:** with junk fees and confusing billing.  
- **Guilt:** leaning on kids or canceling plans.  
- **Envy:** peers with pensions/paid-off homes and cushion.  
- **Sadness:** loss of independence and spontaneity.

#### Solutions explored / tried
**Part-time W-2 / retail shifts**  
- *Like:* predictable hours, simple work.  
- *Hate:* low pay; rigid schedules; energy drain.

**Gig economy (delivery, rideshare, tasks)**  
- *Like:* start/stop flexibility; immediate earnings.  
- *Hate:* physical strain; safety/insurance concerns; thin margins.

**Home-based online tasks / small remote jobs**  
- *Like:* low physical load; stay at home.  
- *Hate:* tech hurdles; scams; inconsistent pay.

**Debt/relief maneuvers (consolidation, hardship plans, reverse mortgage)**  
- *Like:* breathing room; housing stability.  
- *Hate:* fees, complexity, long-term tradeoffs.

**Downsize/asset sales (move, sell items)**  
- *Like:* lowers monthly burden; quick cash.  
- *Hate:* emotional cost; limited upside; logistics.

#### Who they blame
- Rising costs and opaque systems (healthcare/insurance/utilities) that punish fixed incomes.  
- Employers and markets that undervalue older workers.

#### Constraints (real + perceived)
- **Health/energy variability** (can’t commit to strenuous or long shifts).  
- **Fixed-income psychology** (can’t risk upfront costs or delays).  
- **Tech tolerance** (low appetite for new tools).  
- **Transportation limits** (night driving, safety).  
- **Housing immobility** (lease/mortgage; community ties).  
- **Social pride** (avoid dependence on kids/charity).  
- **Trust and simplicity** (must be clear, scam-proof, low admin).

#### Primary promise
> **Create a steady second paycheck—low-strain income that covers rising costs, restores a cushion, and flexes with your health and life.**

#### Likely objections
- “Is this simple enough for me to manage?”  
- “How soon does money arrive—and is it consistent?”  
- “No upfront costs or tech headaches, right?”  
- “What if my health dips or I need to travel?”  
- “Is it safe—no scams, no fine-print surprises?”  
- “Will this affect my benefits or taxes in a bad way?”  
- “I don’t want to bother my kids—can I do this quietly?”  
- “I can’t drive nights or lift heavy—does this still fit?”  
- “What happens if I need to pause?”

#### Hopes & dreams
- **Short term (30–90 days):**  
  - Bills steady; groceries/meds worry fades.  
  - A small, real buffer appears and stays.  
  - Less exhaustion; more good days with family.  
  - Dignity intact—no awkward asks for help.
- **Long term (12–36 months):**  
  - A calm, livable rhythm on a fixed budget.  
  - Medical surprises don’t topple everything.  
  - Home and community stability preserved.  
  - Pride in remaining independent and dependable.

—

## Independence Bucket

### 30s × Independence — “Prime Pivot”

#### Who they are

Early–mid 30s salaried professionals who’ve tasted the limits of mid-level roles and “golden handcuffs.” Commute, meetings, and politics crowd out life; childcare and housing costs squeeze cash flow. They’ve tested side hustles with mixed results and carry healthy skepticism, yet a strong pull toward autonomy. They clicked **Independence** because they want time, location, and income control before life locks in tighter.

#### Core problem (in their words)

* “I’m trading my best hours for someone else’s goals.”
* “My income ceiling and schedule aren’t mine to set.”
* “If I don’t pivot now, I’ll spend my prime years stuck.”

#### Emotional landscape

* **Fear:** wasting prime years on busywork and ceilings.
* **Anger:** at gatekeepers, office politics, and performative culture.
* **Shame:** for quitting past attempts or not making progress.
* **Disgust:** with commute, meetings, and corporate churn.
* **Guilt:** missing time with partner/kids while still feeling broke.
* **Envy:** peers living flexible, remote, self-directed lives.
* **Sadness:** grieving the idealized career that never materialized.

#### Solutions explored / tried

**Traditional career moves (promotions, job hops)**

* *Like:* bigger title, comp bumps, benefits.
* *Hate:* new ceilings, politics, slow change to lifestyle.

**Gig/side hustles (service gigs, micro-freelance)**

* *Like:* quick start, proof of competence.
* *Hate:* inconsistent, time-for-dollars grind; weekends evaporate.

**Online businesses (courses, e-comm, SaaS-y ideas)**

* *Like:* scale potential, location freedom.
* *Hate:* steep learning curves, noise, marketing fatigue.

**Creator path (content, audience-first)**

* *Like:* identity-aligned, optional upside.
* *Hate:* algorithm dependence, long ramp to revenue.

**Investments (index funds, crypto, rentals)**

* *Like:* wealth building, “money working.”
* *Hate:* capital required, volatility, slow impact on monthly cash.

#### Who they blame

* Corporate structures that reward politics over outcomes; cost-of-living creep.
* Themselves for fragmented focus and chasing too many “starts.”

#### Constraints (real + perceived)

* **Benefits tether** (health insurance).
* **Childcare/time scarcity**; evenings already spoken for.
* **Capital limits** (student loans, house-poor dynamics).
* **Focus bandwidth** (context-switching burnout).
* **Consistency gap** (spiky output/income).
* **Belief wobble** (fear of being “just average”).
* **Partner alignment** (risk tolerance, household load).

#### Primary promise

> **Replace your paycheck quickly—then outgrow it—with flexible, schedule-controlled earnings that fit your life now and compound your freedom.**

#### Likely objections

* “I’ve tried before—why will this be different?”
* “I can’t risk benefits or a big income dip.”
* “I don’t have spare hours or mental energy.”
* “I’m not a natural self-promoter.”
* “What if it takes forever to work?”
* “I’m overwhelmed by options—what’s the path?”
* “Will this actually change my day-to-day life?”
* “My partner won’t support another false start.”
* “What if I’m not exceptional enough to stand out?”

#### Hopes & dreams

* **Short term (30–90 days):**

  * Tangible early wins that prove momentum.
  * A calmer schedule with protected personal time.
  * Confidence that the ceiling is breakable.
  * Less dread on Sunday night; more energy for family.
* **Long term (12–36 months):**

  * Location/schedule freedom as the norm.
  * Income matching or surpassing W-2 on their terms.
  * A life designed around values, not meetings.
  * Pride in building something durable and self-directed.

---

### 40s × Independence — “Ceiling Breaker”

#### Who they are

Early to mid-40s professionals/managers with a full plate—mortgage, kids, emerging parent care—who’ve hit role or pay ceilings. Competent and respected, but constrained by meetings, politics, and a calendar they don’t control. Past attempts at autonomy underperformed or stalled. They clicked **Independence** because they want paycheck-level control—on their terms—before it’s “too late.”

#### Core problem (in their words)

* “I’ve earned trust, not freedom.”
* “My income and calendar are capped by other people.”
* “If I don’t act now, the window closes.”

#### Emotional landscape

* **Fear:** becoming permanently stuck or irrelevant.
* **Anger:** at bureaucracy and decision-by-committee.
* **Shame:** for not converting experience into autonomy sooner.
* **Disgust:** with wasted effort and performative urgency.
* **Guilt:** divided attention between kids, spouse, and work.
* **Envy:** peers who control their pipeline and pace.
* **Sadness:** mourning the tradeoffs that never delivered freedom.

#### Solutions explored / tried

**Traditional career moves (lateral/vertical, new firms)**

* *Like:* stability, benefits, reputation lift.
* *Hate:* same ceiling in a new wrapper; politics persists.

**Gig/side income (evenings/weekends)**

* *Like:* quick proof, extra dollars.
* *Hate:* family time sacrificed; volatility and burnout.

**Online businesses (productized services, stores, platforms)**

* *Like:* leverage, potential to replace paycheck.
* *Hate:* complexity creep; long ramp to dependable income.

**Creator/expert branding**

* *Like:* authority, inbound potential.
* *Hate:* content treadmill; inconsistent monetization.

**Investments (real estate, yield, funds)**

* *Like:* wealth pathway, diversification.
* *Hate:* capital needs, risk cycles, slow monthly impact.

#### Who they blame

* Corporate ceilings and shifting goals; economy that punishes midlife pivots.
* Themselves for caution and letting comfort delay action.

#### Constraints (real + perceived)

* **Benefits dependence** (family health coverage).
* **Time squeeze** (kids’ schedules + parent care).
* **Mortgage anchor** (limited geographic flexibility).
* **Reputation risk** (don’t want public missteps).
* **Cognitive load** (already at capacity).
* **Decision fatigue** (too many options, not enough certainty).
* **Spousal alignment** (risk/reward agreement needed).

#### Primary promise

> **Replace your paycheck and break the ceiling for good—dependable, calendar-controlled earnings that protect your family now and scale without cap, on your terms.**

#### Likely objections

* “Show me evidence this becomes dependable, not spiky.”
* “How do I protect benefits and household stability while I shift?”
* “My time is already maxed—what gives?”
* “I’ve built a reputation; I can’t afford public failure.”
* “I won’t drag my family through another experiment.”
* “Where do I focus first—what actually moves the needle?”
* “What if I’m too late to build something meaningful?”
* “Will this truly change my calendar—or just add more work?”
* “What if the payoff doesn’t justify the tradeoffs?”

#### Hopes & dreams

* **Short term (30–90 days):**

  * Clear, confidence-building progress and early wins.
  * Family sees time returning and stress dropping.
  * A path that respects obligations without stalling.
  * Renewal of pride and agency at midlife.
* **Long term (12–36 months):**

  * Paycheck-level autonomy with reliable monthly income.
  * Control of calendar and location without career penalty.
  * Financial resilience and optionality for the family.
  * A legacy of leadership over their own life and time.

—

### 50s × Independence — “Defensive Autonomy”

#### Who they are

Long-tenured W-2 pros or managers who feel a layoff (or forced downgrade) coming. House-rich/cash-tight is common. Retirement looks behind plan. They still have capability, reputation, and a network—but believe remaining an employee leaves them exposed in their last high-earning decade. They click **Independence** because they see control as the only path to safety.

#### Core problem (in their words)

* “I’m at my highest salary and therefore highest risk.”
* “I’ve got 10–12 good earning years left—if I don’t take the wheel now, I’ll be stuck.”
* “Benefits handcuff me to a job that won’t protect me.”

#### Emotional landscape

* **Fear:** sudden layoff, being unhireable at current pay, benefits loss.
* **Anger:** at corporate games, ageism, moving goalposts.
* **Shame:** for “waiting too long,” not saving “enough.”
* **Disgust:** with politics, performative culture, pointless reorgs.
* **Guilt:** for not being more available to family while chasing stability.
* **Envy:** of peers who went independent and control their calendars.
* **Sadness:** grieving a career they outgrew (or that outgrew them).

#### Solutions explored / tried

**Traditional path (stay/ladder/another W-2)**

* *Like:* predictable pay, benefits.
* *Hate:* capped upside, age risk, zero control over reorgs.

**Gig / side income (contract shifts, rideshare, tutoring, etc.)**

* *Like:* quick start, cash now.
* *Hate:* low status/fit; time-for-dollars treadmill; little leverage.

**Online businesses (coaching, e-comm, content, courses)**

* *Like:* autonomy, upside.
* *Hate:* steep learning curves, noisy markets, marketing they don’t want to do.

**“Leverage your expertise” consulting**

* *Like:* uses what they already do; higher perceived value; fewer clients.
* *Hate:* uncertainty landing first client; packaging offers; pricing; selling.

**Investments (index funds, rental, dividends)**

* *Like:* familiar, “responsible.”
* *Hate:* slow to change cash flow; rates/inflation risk; landlord headaches.

#### Who they blame

* Corporations and ageist hiring practices; “the system” (401k myth vs reality).
* Economic whiplash (2008 → COVID → inflation).
* Themselves, a little, for not acting earlier.

#### Constraints (real + perceived)

* **Benefits dependence** (healthcare).
* **Time/energy** (full-time job + family duties).
* **Cash runway** (can’t tolerate long ramp).
* **Tech/marketing aversion** (don’t want to “become a marketer”).
* **Confidence rust** (selling themselves feels foreign).
* **Reputation risk** (moonlighting optics; non-compete worries).
* **Spousal alignment** (risk tolerance mismatch).

#### Primary promise
> Replace layoff anxiety with a controllable, schedule-friendly **income layer** that strengthens your household each month—**without quitting**, gambling your savings, or reinventing who you are.

#### Likely objections
- “If this doesn’t work, I don’t have time to recover.”
- “I can’t risk losing healthcare/benefits.”
- “I don’t have spare hours or mental bandwidth.”
- “I’m not a natural self-promoter.”
- “What if the income is inconsistent?”
- “Will my age work against me?”
- “My spouse won’t support another risky idea.”
- “I don’t want to start over or look foolish.”
- “I need confidentiality.”

#### Hopes & dreams
- **Short term (30–90 days):**
  - Sleep-through-the-night relief; a believable path forward.
  - Early, meaningful wins that prove *I’m back in control*.
  - Keep benefits intact while progress starts.
  - Feel respected and valuable again—at home and at work.
- **Long term (12–36 months):**
  - True optionality: stay or go on *my* terms.
  - Predictable monthly cash flow covering core bills and savings.
  - Confident retirement trajectory; debt shrinking; full emergency fund.
  - More time with family; the power to say “no”; pride and dignity restored.

---

### 60s × Independence — “Self-Reliant Flex”

#### Who they are

Late-career or recently retired professionals who won’t—or can’t—fully stop working. They feel hiring doors closing and fixed income fragile. Energy and health vary; they value dignity, relevance, and not being dependent on employers, government, or family. They click **Independence** because self-reliance feels safer than dependence.

#### Core problem (in their words)

* “Too young to stop, too old to get hired easily.”
* “Fixed income feels fragile—costs keep creeping.”
* “I want income I can **dial up/down** around my life and health.”

#### Emotional landscape

* **Fear:** outliving savings, medical shocks.
* **Anger:** at being sidelined despite experience.
* **Shame:** about asking kids for help or “unretiring.”
* **Disgust:** with ageist hoops and busywork.
* **Guilt:** split time between spouse/family and earning.
* **Envy:** of peers with effortless consulting gigs.
* **Sadness:** loss of daily purpose/community from work.

#### Solutions explored / tried

**Part-time W-2 / contract roles**

* *Like:* familiar, predictable.
* *Hate:* inflexible schedules, younger-manager friction, commute/energy tax.

**Gig / local services (seasonal, delivery, handyman, etc.)**

* *Like:* immediate earning.
* *Hate:* physical strain, low leverage/status, schedule control is fake.

**Online side plays (reselling, e-comm, content)**

* *Like:* home-based, flexible.
* *Hate:* tech sprawl, slow ramp, constant marketing.

**Micro-consulting/advisory**

* *Like:* high trust, phone/Zoom based, uses career capital.
* *Hate:* don’t know how to productize; fear of selling; feast/famine.

**Income investments (yield, rentals)**

* *Like:* passive-leaning idea.
* *Hate:* capital required; volatility; landlord risk; not “now cash.”

#### Who they blame

* Employers/market for age bias; policymakers for cost pressures.
* Themselves a bit for not planning better—but they’re focused on solutions.

#### Constraints (real + perceived)

* **Health/energy variance** (can’t promise 50–60 hr weeks).
* **Tech tolerance** (low appetite for tools/learning curves).
* **Fixed-income psychology** (loss-aversion; hates upfront costs).
* **Spousal harmony** (schedule, stress, risk).
* **Network freshness** (contacts retired/moved).
* **Confidence in selling** (wary of being “pushy”).

#### Primary promise
> **Build real independence with a calm, schedule-friendly income you control—flexible to your energy and life, reliable enough to protect savings and quiet money worries.**

#### Likely objections
- “I don’t want to wrestle with tech or complicated systems.”
- “What if my health or energy dips?”
- “I can’t tolerate big upfront costs or long ramps.”
- “Do I still have relevant value people will pay for?”
- “I don’t want to sell or chase strangers.”
- “Will this actually help—or just keep me busy?”
- “My spouse wants less stress, not more.”
- “What if I become dependent on something I can’t keep up with?”

#### Hopes & dreams
- **Short term (30–90 days):**
  - Feel useful and in control again; money anxiety softens.
  - A simple, repeatable rhythm that fits my days.
  - Respect and autonomy without rigid schedules.
  - Room for health, family, and travel while still contributing.
- **Long term (12–36 months):**
  - A calm, flexible life where work is optional and on my terms.
  - A financial cushion that makes retirement feel safe—no fear of outliving savings.
  - Freedom from gatekeepers; able to help family without strain.
  - A legacy of self-reliance and generosity—remembered as steady, wise, dependable.

The Revolutionary 5 Step Process
For Uncovering the Hidden BIG Ideas That
Your Market is Powerless to Resist
Breakthrough Ideas
1

Today we’re going to talk about coming up with BIG Ideas

And I’m going to give you a brand-new framework for finding Big Ideas in 60 minutes or less. 

Sound good?

So what is a Big Idea, anyways?

Here’s how legendary advertiser David Ogilvy defines a big idea:
 
A big idea is an idea that is instantly comprehended as important, exciting, and beneficial. It also leads to an inevitable conclusion, a conclusion that makes it easy to sell your product.

Let’s look at some recent examples of big ideas I’ve used for myself and my clients.

Okay, so the questions I want to answer today are:
 
1. “How did I come up with these Big Ideas?” 
 
2. And how can you do the same thing, no matter what you sell or how you sell it?

So let’s jump right in!

Part I. What Kind Of Ideas Are We Looking For?

The way I see it there are 3 main phases of an idea…

Our Thesis: 
The sweet spot is when you find ideas that are primed to make the leap from hidden to emerging. 

We’ll talk about what ‘Primed’ means in a minute…

But first:

Why do we want to focus on Hidden Ideas vs. Emerging or Known Ideas?

There are more Hidden Ideas than there are Emerging Ideas or Known Ideas.

P
G
S
Emerging Ideas and Known Ideas are very finite data sets. 
 
There are only so many Known Ideas. 
 
Meanwhile, for any category or problem we only see a few Emerging Ideas at any given time. 

In contrast, there are a near-infinite number of Hidden Ideas…

Ideas that have yet to be “discovered,” or that are only known to a handful of individuals. 

Additionally, because Hidden Ideas haven’t even begun to penetrate the mainstream, there is significantly LESS resistance to them. They are harder to refute or disprove. 

And finally, Hidden Ideas are more exciting. People love feeling like they are on the cutting-edge of innovation (we’ll discuss more about this shortly). 

Part II. How Do You Find Hidden Ideas?

7 Parts: 

Formulate
Rephrase
Search
Modify
Categorize
Organize
Rank

1. Formulate the problem you are trying to solve. 
 

Hint: every idea solves a problem. 
The idea of gravity came from the problem of not being able to mathematically explain why we don’t float off into space. 
Start in very broad terms.

The problem I am trying to solve is: 
Type II Diabetes
Millennials being broke
Seniors not having enough money for retirement. 
Busy professionals failing to find love. 
Problem-Drinking
Self-Sabotage
Can’t Hit A Driver Straight

2. Rephrase it as a “WHY” question:
 

Why do people get Type II Diabetes?
Why are so many millennials broke?
Why do many seniors not have enough money for retirement?
Why do some busy professionals fail to find love?  
Why do people have drinking problems?
Why do people self-sabotage?
Why Can’t I Hit My Driver Straight?

3. Search For Answers  

Where to Look: 
Google Search
Google News
NCBI
Databases (Science Daily)
Forums
Podcasts
YouTube

4. Modify: Add Simple Modifiers to Your Search: 
 

Surprising

Breakthrough

New Discovery
New Research

New Data

New Finding

Shocking

Real Reason
Science Behind

IMPORTANT
For Steps 3 and 4…
 
When you find interesting answers to your searches (aka potential Hidden Ideas), don’t be afraid to dig deeper. Write down your first notes and impressions. Ask if this idea you’ve uncovered could be related to other ideas and investigate if it is. Don’t be afraid to ask “Why” more times or peel back the layers of the onion. 

5. Categorize
 
Spend 30-60 minutes and list out every answer you find. For each one, categorize it as:

1. Hidden

2. Emerging

3. Known

6. Organize
 
Set anything that appears to be an emerging idea, or a known idea, off to the side (we’ll come back to these)... 

7. Rank
 
Rank your potential Hidden Ideas based on your gut reaction to how interesting or powerful they seem. 

Our Thesis: 
The sweet spot is when you find ideas that are primed to make the leap from hidden to emerging. 

Example: Seniors not having enough money for retirement.

1. Formulate the problem you are trying to solve. 
 
The Problem I’m Trying to Solve Is:
Seniors not having enough money for retirement.

2. Rephrase as a “Why” question:
 
Why do many seniors not have enough money for retirement?

3. Search For Answers
 

Literally the First Result:

“But there is no county in the U.S. where the average cost of living and the average Social Security benefit are equal, which makes it harder for retirees to break even.”

Remember:
For Steps 3 and 4…
 
When you find interesting answers to your searches (aka potential Hidden Ideas), don’t be afraid to dig deeper. Write down your first notes and impressions. Ask if this idea you’ve uncovered could be related to other ideas and investigate if it is. Don’t be afraid to ask “Why” more times or peel back the layers of the onion. 

My Immediate Ideas or Thoughts:
 
See-Saw
Tipped Scales
No Equilibrium
Disparity
What are other examples of where things are disproportionate or out of balance?

Now I Keep Reading the Article:

“Those individuals have incomes that are below a measure the researchers have developed called the Elder Index.

The Elder Index measures the basic costs households face and how well they are able to meet those expenses. That includes food, housing, health care, transportation and other necessities. It does not cover extras such as entertainment, vacations or dining out.”

Now I Keep Reading the Article:

The tally for the Elder Index varies based on an individual’s or couple’s situation. For those who no longer have to pay a mortgage on their home, the Elder Index is $21,012 annually for an individual and $31,800 for a couple.

Those estimates go up for those who rent — rising to $25,416 for singles and $36,204 for couples. And costs are even higher for those who are still paying off their mortgages — $32,064 for singles and $42,852 for couples.

The Elder Index is an interesting concept. I haven’t heard of this. Can we play with it?

What about mortgage issues?

Headline Idea: 
 
“The Great American Homeownership Myth That’s Crushing Senior Citizens”.

Could we do a whole promo around the idea that seniors were sold home ownership as the American Dream and now it’s the American Nightmare because their mortgages are one of the top factors keeping them from retiring?

Could we have a financial promo around how the coming crisis with our seniors isn’t about social security, but about how they’ll be losing their homes?

What about something around how the next great housing crisis will start with evicted seniors?

That’s scary and we know fear sells…
But we can actually flip it and position it as an opportunity…

By showing how the companies that are solving the so-called senior housing crisis could yield you a huge  windfall if you invest in them. In fact, making these investments today could even make sure you stay in your house in the decades to come. 
 

My Next Step

Okay, so now I’m going to Google the term “Senior Housing Crisis” and see if it’s a big thing?

Reonomy (2017)

Reonomy (2017)

“The lack of affordable housing for the growing senior population in the United States is on the verge of becoming a crisis. The country is not prepared to meet the housing needs of this aging group. Adults in the U.S. aged 50 and over is expected to grow to 133 million by 2030, an increase of more than 70 percent since 2000 according to a study by Harvard.”

Forbes (May, 2021)

Forbes (May, 2021)

Yet this all-important element of our lives remains at risk for a population most vulnerable in its absence: seniors. Known as the "silver tsunami," the number of Americans aged 65 or older is projected to surpass 72 million in 2030 and 83 million in 2050. This significant demographic trend places substantial pressure on a system already experiencing a shortage of senior housing, which supplements supportive services with residences. As our population ages, we face one of the greatest crises of our time, one seemingly lost among concerns over the pandemic and global warming, yet one nevertheless present and lurking.

New York Times (September, 2020)

American Bar Association (January, 2020)

Minnesota Housing Partnership (Date Unknown)

So to me, an immediate potential Hidden Idea I could use: 
Expensive mortgages are putting an unprecedented number of seniors at risk of losing their homes, while creating a once-in-a-lifetime investment opportunity.

Now let’s look at other search results that came up:

Employers have phased out pensions.
Didn’t take advantage of IRAs or work-based 401ks

Google Page 1, Modified Search Result

Google Pay 1 Modified Search Result

As employers contend with growing numbers of younger employees quitting in the great resignation, the COVID-19 recession and gradual labor market recovery has also been accompanied by an increase in retirement among adults ages 55 and older.

It is unclear whether the pandemic-induced increase in retirement among older adults will be temporary or longer lasting. Newly published labor force projections from the Bureau of Labor Statistics suggest it will be temporary. BLS projects large increases in labor force participation among older adults from 2020 to 2030, with nearly 40% of 65- to 69-year-olds being in the labor force by 2030, up from 33% in 2020.

A second Hidden Idea:

More seniors have retired because they got richer during the pandemic, and their assets increased. But it might be temporary because with a recession or big economic event, they’ll be dragged back into the workforce.

Google Page 1, Modified Search Result

A third Hidden Idea:

The Growing Retirement Wealth Gap (And How To Make Sure You’re On The Winning Side)

Spend 30-60 minutes and list out every answer you find. For each one, categorize it as:

1. Hidden

2. Emerging

3. Known

5. Categorize

Hidden Ideas:
 
Expensive mortgages are putting an unprecedented number of seniors at risk of losing their homes, while creating a once-in-a-lifetime investment opportunity.

Covid-19 Created A Growing Retirement Wealth Gap (And How To Make Sure You’re On The Winning Side)

Continuing to Support Adult Children for Longer is Keeping Seniors in the Workforce

Phased Out Pensions

Not Enough Money (Or No Money) In IRAs or 401ks

Supporting Family in General

Hidden Idea #1: 
Expensive mortgages are putting an unprecedented number of seniors at risk of losing their homes, while creating a once-in-a-lifetime investment opportunity. 
 
Hidden Idea #2:
Covid-19 Accelerated Seniors Retiring But It May Be Temporary (And You Could Be At Risk)
 
Hidden Idea #3:
Covid-19 Created A Growing Retirement Wealth Gap 

Part III_A: 
How Do You Decide If A Hidden Idea Is In The “Sweet Spot?”
 

Our Thesis: 
The sweet spot is when you find ideas that are primed to make the leap from hidden to emerging. 

Two Criteria: Momentum and Virality

Momentum
 
For an idea to be worth us pursuing, it either needs to already have some organic momentum…

Or, the right conditions need to be in place where we can manufacture the appearance of momentum.  

In other words, we want to be able to point to a handful of new studies, data points, or articles, that are coming out in support of our Hidden Idea, and use those as evidence that our idea has momentum. We want to create the argument that people are already talking about this idea, and that it’s poised to emerge into the mainstream. 

When a Sweet Spot Idea has organic momentum, you’ll see AT LEAST one of the following:
 
1. A small cluster of related studies or papers all being published in near succession, that build upon or reference one-another, and that all support the emergence of a new discovery

Example: 
3 new scientific studies published in NCBI in 2019…
2 new scientific studies published in NCBI in 2020…
4 new scientific studies published in NCBI in 2021…
All supporting our Hidden Idea. 

When a Sweet Spot Idea has organic momentum, you’ll see AT LEAST one of the following:
 
2. A small cluster of data being published near-simultaneously that all appear to confirm the emergence of a new truth about the world. 

Example:
2 new Public Opinion Polls published by PEW Research in 2019…
4 new Public Opinion Polls published by PEW Research in 2020…
3 new Public Opinion Polls published by PEW Research in 2021…
All supporting our Hidden Idea. 

When a Sweet Spot Idea has organic momentum, you’ll see AT LEAST one of the following:
 
3. A small cluster of articles, blogs, or news stories being published near-simultaneously that all suggest the emergence of a new cultural moment, pattern, shift, or trend. 

Example: 
2 new Mainstream News Articles Published in 2019…
5 new Mainstream News Articles Published in 2020…
4 Mainstream News Articles Published in 2021…
All supporting our Hidden Idea. 

Visually it looks like this…
 

IMPORTANT

Note that the choice of wording for a “small cluster” is very important and intentional. I don’t want dozens of studies, articles, or data sets. I want a few credible ones. The reason why is because we want to find ideas that are UNKNOWN. Meaning that if I were to ask 10 random people in the street if they’d heard of an idea, at least 8/10 would say “no.” Ideally, we actually want 10/10 lay people to have not heard of the idea before. 
 

Example: Seniors not having enough money for retirement.
 

Hidden Idea #1: 
Expensive mortgages are putting an unprecedented number of seniors at risk of losing their homes, while creating a once-in-a-lifetime investment opportunity. 
 

Does this idea have organic momentum?
 

CNBC Article (2020)

Reonomy Article (2017)

Forbes Article (2021)

NYT Article (2020)

American Bar Association Article (2020)

MHP Article

Plus, compelling data from Harvard
 

Does this idea have organic momentum?
 

YES!

This Hidden Idea appears to have the right amount of momentum!
 

Things are happening and people, institutions, and the media are already talking about it…

But it’s not something that is all over the news or on the forefront of the average person’s mind, and it’s not a major part of the national conversation yet. 

It feels like the first embers or sparks are in place and that with the right kindling this idea could catch-fire. 

If we search Google News for “Senior Housing Crisis” most of what comes up is unrelated stories about affordable housing in general, or municipal bonds. (That’s a good thing from our perspective).
 

 

An important note on “momentum”
 

The word momentum can seem kind of “arbitrary”...

But your job is to convince the world (or at least your target market), that this idea is already making the leap from hidden to emerging…

While giving your audience the opportunity to latch-on to this idea as it takes off. 

Q: Why do you need to convince your audience or target market that the idea you are presenting to them is already making the leap from hidden to emerging?
 

A: Most people are followers, and we want to sell to the most people. 

 

Citation: https://www.appcues.com/blog/the-product-adoption-curve

People think they want to be pioneers (innovators)…
 

But none of them really want to risk an arrow in the back. 

In Product Adoption Curve Parlance…
 

Most people really want to be “early adopters” or the “early majority.”

Why?
 

It’s socially risky to be a true innovator. 

What if you’re wrong? What if you look foolish? What if others follow your lead and get hurt? 

The risk of social capital is too great for most people to innovate. 

More Reasons You Want To Create a Picture of Unstoppable Momentum For Your Idea: 
 

People hate missing out. They don’t want to look foolish or to be irrelevant. 

People are excited by new discoveries. There’s a fundamental human curiosity. 

Just as important, people like looking smart. They like being able to share and tell others about a new discovery and take the credit for being on the cutting-edge. 

Part III_B: 
How Do You Decide If A Hidden Idea Is In The “Sweet Spot?”
 

Virality
 
In addition to being supportive of a narrative of momentum…

The idea you select should contain as many of the elements of Virality as possible.

Why Virality?
 
We’re wired for Gossip. We love learning new concepts. We love to share. We love looking smart. 

The Most Important Elements of Virality I’ve Found:
 

Easy to understand – intuitive, you can grasp it, visual (metaphor-friendly)

Emotional – idea is shocking, scary, or exciting and people want to share. 

Curiosity-inducing. 

Contrarian or Paradigm Shifting

Provable – supporting data, social proof, mental test, physical test

Example: Seniors not having enough money for retirement.
 

Hidden Idea #1: 
Expensive mortgages are putting an unprecedented number of seniors at risk of losing their homes, while creating a once-in-a-lifetime investment opportunity. 
 

How does it stand up to our virality checklist?
 

Easy to understand – intuitive, you can grasp it, visual (metaphor-friendly)

Easy to understand that seniors are at risk of losing their homes. Metaphor of scales tipping, or a lack of balance. Metaphor or analogy of previous housing crisis and collapse.  

Emotional – idea is shocking, scary, or exciting and people want to share. 

Scary - what if you or your loved ones will lose their home? 
Exciting - opportunity to cash in on the next big windfall. 

How does it stand up to our virality checklist?
 
Curiosity-inducing.

Curiosity about which companies can solve this crisis and the opportunity to profit. 

How does it stand up to our virality checklist?
 

Contrarian or Paradigm Shifting

Paradigm shifting because instead of having you being at a place of fear about losing your home, getting you to a place of excitement about keeping your home while supporting a company/companies doing good, and make a potential windfall. 

Also paradigm shifting and contrarian because we’re saying the American Dream is actually the American Nightmare for seniors and mortgages are one of the #1 things putting them at risk of never retiring. 

How does it stand up to our virality checklist?
 
Provable
Tons of proof, Harvard Data, articles, studies, press, etc. 

Does the Hidden Idea stand up to our virality checklist?

 

YES!

Not sure if your idea is viral?

Take your Hidden Idea and go explain it to 5 different people. Not other copywriters or marketers, but friends and family. Maybe a stranger at a bar. See how they react to it. Does it seem like they are responding in ways consistent with our Viral checklist we’ve now discussed.

109

Part IV. Deciding If You Have a Sweet Spot Idea
 
If your top ranked Hidden Idea appears to have a strong degree of momentum AND virality, you can move forward with it. 

If it doesn’t, or you have several very exciting Hidden Ideas and can’t decide which one you like best, run your additional Hidden Ideas through the Momentum and Virality test, and choose the hidden idea that seems to have the most organic momentum + checks off the most boxes when it comes to virality. 

Our Thesis: 
The sweet spot is when you find ideas that are primed to make the leap from hidden to emerging. 

Part V. Making Your Sweet Spot Idea Even More Compelling
 
Okay so you’ve got your idea, but how do you make it even better and more compelling? How do you create a narrative of unstoppable viral momentum? Something you can use as your Big Idea going forward?

1. Expand.  Write out your current Sweet Spot Idea and make an argument for why it has momentum.
 

2. Synthesize.  Strengthen the narrative around your Sweet Spot Idea by leveraging the credibility of Emerging or Known Ideas that are both related AND unrelated to your Hidden Idea .

3. Finalize.  Re-summarize as your Big Idea in a final, simplified way.

Example: Seniors not having enough money for retirement.
 

Sweet Spot Idea: 
Expensive mortgages are putting an unprecedented number of seniors at risk of losing their homes, while creating a once-in-a-lifetime investment opportunity. 
 

1. Expand.  Write out your current Sweet Spot Idea and make an argument for why it has momentum.
 

Sweet Spot Idea:
Expensive mortgages are putting an unprecedented number of seniors at risk of losing their homes, while creating a once-in-a-lifetime investment opportunity. Everyone from the AARP to Harvard is talking about it. More of the press is finally waking up to this crisis and data shows that in the coming decade it’s only going to become worse. 

 

2. Synthesize.  

Expensive mortgages are putting an unprecedented number of seniors at risk of losing their homes, while creating a once-in-a-lifetime investment opportunity. Everyone from the AARP to Harvard is talking about it. More of the press is finally waking up to this crisis and data shows that in the coming decade it’s only going to become worse. This comes as seniors are already having a harder time retiring due to poor investing or financial planning, phased out pensions, increased needs to support adult children, and not having enough money saved. All of these things are tipping what is called the Elder Index irreversibly to one side. Not only that, but while Covid-19 accelerated retirement for some of the “haves” those retirements may be temporary, as the great resignation and the great retirement are putting an unprecedented strain on our economy, and that’s before you get to inflation. There’s a ticking time bomb here. But also an unprecedented opportunity for investors. 

3. Finalize.  Re-summarize as your Big Idea in a simple way. 
 

From American Dream to American Nightmare! 
 
Why “Geriatric Mortgages” May Be About to Put 30 Million+ Seniors Out Onto The Street This Decade. 
 
Plus, why investing in these three little-known companies working to stop the crisis before it explodes could lead to unprecedented returns…
 
And ensure your sunset years are DRIPPING with gold. 

Congratulations, You Have Your Big Idea!
